href="/wiki/1987_Rheindahlen_bombing" title="1987 Rheindahlen bombing">Rheindahlen bombing (Germany)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Operation_Flavius" title="Operation Flavius">Operation Flavius (Gibraltar)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/1988_IRA_attacks_in_the_Netherlands" title="1988 IRA attacks in the Netherlands">1988 Netherlands attacks</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Glamorgan_Barracks_bombing" title="Glamorgan Barracks bombing">Glamorgan Barracks bombing (Germany)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Killings_of_Nick_Spanos_and_Stephen_Melrose" title="Killings of Nick Spanos and Stephen Melrose">Roermond killings (Netherlands)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Osnabr%C3%BCck_mortar_attack" title="Osnabr\xc3\xbcck mortar attack">Osnabr\xc3\xbcck barracks (Germany)</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p><b>The Troubles</b> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">Na Triobl\xc3\xb3id\xc3\xad</i>) was an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nationalism" title="Ethnic nationalism">ethno-nationalist</a><sup id="cite_ref-CMitchell_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMitchell-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> conflict in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> during the late 20th century. Also known internationally as the <b>Northern Ireland conflict</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Gloss_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gloss-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and the <b>Conflict in Ireland</b><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> it is sometimes described as a "<a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla war</a>" or a "low-level war".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The conflict began in the late 1960s and is usually deemed to have ended with the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> of 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Ireland_1999,_page_221_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ireland_1999,_page_221-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-elliot_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elliot-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Although the Troubles primarily took place in Northern Ireland, at times the violence spilled over into parts of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">mainland Europe</a>.\n</p><p>The conflict was primarily political and nationalistic, fuelled by historical events.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It also had an ethnic or sectarian dimension,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> although it was not a <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">religious conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CMitchell_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMitchell-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RJenkins_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RJenkins-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> A key issue was the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">constitutional status of Northern Ireland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">Unionists</a>/<a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">loyalists</a>, who were mostly <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestants" title="Ulster Protestants">Protestants</a>, wanted Northern Ireland to remain within the United Kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">Irish nationalists</a>/<a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">republicans</a>, who were mostly <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholics" title="Irish Catholics">Catholics</a>, wanted Northern Ireland to leave the United Kingdom and join a <a href="/wiki/United_Ireland" title="United Ireland">united Ireland</a>.\n</p><p>The conflict began during a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">campaign to end discrimination</a> against the Catholic/nationalist minority by the Protestant/unionist government and police force.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dbryan_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dbryan-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The authorities attempted to suppress this protest campaign and were accused of <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a>; it was also met with violence from loyalists, who alleged it was a republican front. Increasing inter-communal violence, and conflict between nationalist youths and police, eventually led to <a href="/wiki/1969_Northern_Ireland_riots" title="1969 Northern Ireland riots">riots in August 1969</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Banner" title="Operation Banner">deployment of British troops</a>. Some Catholics initially welcomed the army as a more neutral force, but it soon came to be seen as hostile and biased.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The emergence of armed <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary organisations</a> led to the subsequent warfare over the next three decades.\n</p><p>The main <a href="/wiki/Directory_of_the_Northern_Ireland_Troubles" class="mw-redirect" title="Directory of the Northern Ireland Troubles">participants in the Troubles</a> were republican paramilitaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA) and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> (INLA); loyalist paramilitaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> (UVF) and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Association" title="Ulster Defence Association">Ulster Defence Association</a> (UDA); British state security forces \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a> (RUC); and political activists and politicians. The security forces of the Republic played a smaller role. Republican paramilitaries carried out a guerrilla campaign against the British security forces, as well as a bombing campaign against infrastructure, commercial and political targets. Loyalists targeted republicans/nationalists, and attacked the wider Catholic community in what they claimed was retaliation. At times there were bouts of sectarian tit-for-tat violence. The British security forces undertook both a policing and a <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> role, primarily against republicans. There were some incidents of collusion between British security forces and loyalists. The Troubles also involved numerous riots, mass protests and acts of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a>, and led to <a href="/wiki/Segregation_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Segregation in Northern Ireland">segregation</a> and the creation of <a href="/wiki/No-go_area" title="No-go area">no-go areas</a>.\n</p><p>More than 3,500 people were killed in the conflict, of whom 52% were civilians, 32% were members of the British security forces, and 16% were members of paramilitary groups.<sup id="cite_ref-suttonstatus_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonstatus-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> There has been sporadic violence since the Good Friday Agreement was signed, including <a href="/wiki/Dissident_Irish_Republican_campaign" title="Dissident Irish Republican campaign">a campaign by anti-ceasefire republicans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ireland_1999,_page_221_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ireland_1999,_page_221-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-elliot_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elliot-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li allegations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Parades_issue"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Parades issue</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Social_repercussions"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Social repercussions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Casualties"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Casualties</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Responsibility"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Responsibility</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Status"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Status</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Location"><span class="tocnumber">12.3</span> <span class="toctext">Location</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Chronological_listing"><span class="tocnumber">12.4</span> <span class="toctext">Chronological listing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Additional_statistics"><span class="tocnumber">12.5</span> <span class="toctext">Additional statistics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">In popular culture</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountain_(04).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/220px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/330px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/440px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4099" data-file-height="2732" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountain_(04).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>August 2009: the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Banner" title="Ulster Banner">Ulster Banner</a> flying over a <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionist</a> area (foreground) of <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">Irish tricolour</a> flying over a <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">nationalist</a> area (background) of the same city</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg/220px-Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg/330px-Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg/440px-Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg 2x" data-file-width="858" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_peace_line_Cupar_Way.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "<a href="/wiki/Peace_lines" title="Peace lines">peace line</a>" in Belfast. The peace lines are a series of high <a href="/wiki/Border_barriers" class="mw-redirect" title="Border barriers">barriers</a> in Northern Ireland that separate nationalist and unionist neighbourhoods. They have been built at urban <a href="/wiki/Interface_area" title="Interface area">interface areas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>, Derry, <a href="/wiki/Portadown" title="Portadown">Portadown</a> and elsewhere. The stated purpose of the peace lines is to minimise inter-communal violence.</div></div></div>\n<p>"The Troubles" refers to the three-decade conflict between <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">nationalists</a> (mainly self-identified as Irish or Roman Catholic) and <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionists</a> (mainly self-identified as\nBritish or Protestant). The term "the Troubles" was previously used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_revolutionary_period" title="Irish revolutionary period">Irish revolutionary period</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-cottrell_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cottrell-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> it was adopted to refer to the escalating violence in Northern Ireland after 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-storey_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-storey-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The violence was characterised by the armed campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Irish republican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">Ulster loyalist</a> paramilitary groups and British state security forces (the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a> (RUC)). It thus became the focus for the longest major campaign in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_Army" title="History of the British Army">history of the British Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_government" class="mw-redirect" title="British government">British government</a>\'s position is that its forces were neutral in the conflict, trying to uphold law and order in Northern Ireland and the right of the people of Northern Ireland to democratic self-determination. Nationalists regard the state forces as forces of occupation or partisan <a href="/wiki/Combatants" class="mw-redirect" title="Combatants">combatants</a> in the conflict. The British security forces focused on republican paramilitaries and activists, and the "Ballast" investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Police_Ombudsman" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Ombudsman">Police Ombudsman</a> confirmed that British forces colluded on several occasions with loyalist paramilitaries, were involved in murder, and furthermore obstructed the course of justice when claims of collusion and murder were investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-Ballast_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ballast-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Troubles were brought to an uneasy end by a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">peace process</a> that included the declaration of ceasefires by most paramilitary organisations, the complete decommissioning of the IRA\'s weapons, the reform of the police, and the corresponding withdrawal of the British Army from the streets and sensitive <a href="/wiki/Irish_border" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish border">Irish border</a> areas such as <a href="/wiki/County_Armagh" title="County Armagh">South Armagh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fermanagh" title="Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a>, as agreed by the signatories to the <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Belfast Agreement">Belfast Agreement</a> (commonly known as the "Good Friday Agreement"). One part of the Agreement is that Northern Ireland will remain within the United Kingdom unless a majority of the Northern Irish electorate vote otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliament_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliament-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> It also established the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Executive" title="Northern Ireland Executive">Northern Ireland Executive</a>, a devolved power-sharing government, which must consist of both unionist and nationalist parties.\n</p><p>Although the number of active participants was relatively small, the Troubles affected many in Northern Ireland on a daily basis; their impact sometimes spread to England and the Republic of Ireland, and, occasionally, to parts of mainland Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-holliday_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holliday-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="1609.E2.80.931791"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1609\xe2\x80\x931791">1609\xe2\x80\x931791</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1609\xe2\x80\x931791">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_(Ierland)_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III,_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg/220px-De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg/330px-De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg/440px-De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_%28Ierland%29_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III%2C_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2952" data-file-height="2004" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:De_slag_aan_de_Boyne_(Ierland)_tussen_Jacobus_II_en_Willem_III,_12_juli_1690_Rijksmuseum_SK-A-605.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Boyne" title="Battle of the Boyne">Battle of the Boyne</a> (12 July 1690) by <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Huchtenburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan van Huchtenburg">Jan van Huchtenburg</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1609, Scottish and English <a href="/wiki/Settler" title="Settler">settlers</a>, known as <i>planters</i>, were given land escheated from the native Irish in the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">Plantation of Ulster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Coupled with Protestant immigration to "unplanted" areas of Ulster, particularly Antrim and Down, this resulted in conflict between the native Catholics and the "planters", leading in turn to two bloody religious conflicts known as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Confederate_Wars" title="Irish Confederate Wars">Irish Confederate Wars</a> (1641\xe2\x80\x931653) and the <a href="/wiki/Williamite_war_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Williamite war in Ireland">Williamite war</a> (1689\xe2\x80\x931691), both of which resulted in Protestant victories.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> dominance in Ireland was ensured by the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a> that curtailed the religious, legal, and political rights of anyone (including both Catholics and Protestant Dissenters, such as <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterians</a>) who did not conform to the state church, the Anglican <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>. As the Penal Laws started to be phased out in the latter part of the 18th century, there was more competition for land, as restrictions were lifted on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Irish Catholic</a> ability to rent. With Roman Catholics allowed to buy land and enter trades from which they had formerly been banned, tensions arose resulting in the Protestant "<a href="/wiki/Peep_O%27Day_Boys" class="mw-redirect" title="Peep O&#39;Day Boys">Peep O\'Day Boys</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and Catholic "<a href="/wiki/Defenders_(Ireland)" title="Defenders (Ireland)">Defenders</a>". This created polarisation between the communities and a dramatic reduction in reformers among Protestants, many of whom had been growing more receptive to democratic reform.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1791.E2.80.931912"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1791\xe2\x80\x931912">1791\xe2\x80\x931912</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1791\xe2\x80\x931912">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Following the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_republican" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish republican">republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_United_Irishmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Society of the United Irishmen">Society of the United Irishmen</a> by Presbyterians, Catholics, and liberal Anglicans, and the resulting failed <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, sectarian violence between Catholics and Protestants continued. The <a href="/wiki/Orange_Institution" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange Institution">Orange Order</a> (founded 1795), with its stated goal of upholding the Protestant faith and loyalty to the heirs of <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William of Orange</a>, dates from this period and remains active to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the 1801 <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Act of Union</a>, a new political framework was formed with the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish Parliament</a> and incorporation of Ireland into the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>. The result was a closer tie between Anglicans and the formerly republican Presbyterians as part of a "loyal" Protestant community. Although <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a> was achieved in 1829, largely eliminating official discrimination against Roman Catholics (then around 75% of Ireland\'s population), Dissenters, and Jews, the <a href="/wiki/Repeal_Association" title="Repeal Association">Repeal Association</a>\'s campaign to repeal the 1801 Union failed.\n</p><p>In the late 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule movement">Home Rule movement</a> was created and served to define the divide between most nationalists (usually Catholics), who sought the restoration of an Irish Parliament, and most unionists (usually Protestants), who were afraid of being a minority under a Catholic-dominated Irish Parliament and who tended to support continuing union with Britain.\n</p><p>Unionists and Home Rule advocates were the main political factions in late 19th- and early 20th-century Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1912.E2.80.931922"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1912\xe2\x80\x931922">1912\xe2\x80\x931922</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1912\xe2\x80\x931922">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster_Covenant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulster_Covenant.jpg/220px-Ulster_Covenant.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulster_Covenant.jpg/330px-Ulster_Covenant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Ulster_Covenant.jpg/440px-Ulster_Covenant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1460" data-file-height="1904" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster_Covenant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Ulster_Covenant" title="Ulster Covenant">Ulster Covenant</a></i> was issued in protest against the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Act_1914" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule Act 1914">Third Home Rule Bill</a> in September 1912.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/220px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/330px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/440px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 2x" data-file-width="954" data-file-height="1450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_Irish_Republic" title="Proclamation of the Irish Republic">Proclamation of the Irish Republic</a></i> was issued during the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> of April 1916.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Partition of Ireland</a></div>\n<p>By the second decade of the 20th century, Home Rule, or limited Irish self-government, was on the brink of being conceded due to the agitation of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a>. \nIn response to the campaign for Home Rule which started in the 1870s, unionists, mostly Protestant and largely concentrated in Ulster, had resisted both self-government and independence for Ireland, fearing for their future in an overwhelmingly Catholic country dominated by the Roman Catholic Church. In 1912, unionists led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Edward Carson</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Covenant" title="Ulster Covenant">Ulster Covenant</a> and pledged to resist Home Rule by force if necessary. To this end, they formed the paramilitary <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> (UVF).<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In response, nationalists led by <a href="/wiki/Eoin_MacNeill" title="Eoin MacNeill">Eoin MacNeill</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a>, whose goal was to oppose the UVF and ensure enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Act_1914" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule Act 1914">Third Home Rule Bill</a> in the event of British or unionist recalcitrance. The outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> in 1914 and <a href="/wiki/Ireland_and_World_War_I" title="Ireland and World War I">Ireland\'s involvement in the war</a> temporarily averted possible civil war in Ireland and delayed the resolution of the question of Irish independence. Home Rule, although passed in the British Parliament with <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a>, was suspended for the duration of the war.\n</p><p>The Irish Volunteers split, with a majority, called <a href="/wiki/National_Volunteers" title="National Volunteers">National Volunteers</a>, joining Irish regiments of the <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">New British Army</a>. Many of those who stayed were radical nationalists, among them <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Brotherhood" title="Irish Republican Brotherhood">Irish Republican Brotherhood</a> infiltrators. From these ranks came those who launched the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> in Dublin in 1916, led by <a href="/wiki/Padraig_Pearse" class="mw-redirect" title="Padraig Pearse">Padraig Pearse</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Connolly" title="James Connolly">James Connolly</a>. Two and a half years after the executions of fifteen of the Rising\'s leaders, the separatist <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> party won the December 1918 <a href="/wiki/Irish_general_election,_1918" title="Irish general election, 1918">Irish general election</a> with 48% of the vote and a majority of seats, and set up the 1919 <a href="/wiki/First_D%C3%A1il" title="First D\xc3\xa1il">First D\xc3\xa1il</a> (Irish Parliament) in Dublin. Their victory was aided by the <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">threat of conscription for First World War service</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_for_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish War for Independence">Irish War for Independence</a> followed, leading to eventual independence in 1922 for the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, which comprised 26 of the 32 Irish counties. In <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, particularly in the six counties which became <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, Sinn F\xc3\xa9in fared relatively poorly in the 1918 election, and unionists won a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a> partitioned the island of Ireland into two separate jurisdictions, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%931922)" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x931922)">Southern Ireland</a> and Northern Ireland, both devolved regions of the United Kingdom. This <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">partition of Ireland</a> was confirmed when the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a> exercised its right in December 1922 under the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> of 1921 to <i>"opt out"</i> of the newly established Irish Free State.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliament_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliament-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A part of the treaty signed in 1922 mandated that a boundary commission would sit to decide where the frontier of the northern state would be in relation to its southern neighbour. After the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Irish Civil War</a> of 1922\xe2\x80\x931923, this part of the treaty was given less priority by the new Dublin government led by <a href="/wiki/W._T._Cosgrave" title="W. T. Cosgrave">W. T. Cosgrave</a>, and was quietly dropped. As counties <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Tyrone" title="County Tyrone">Tyrone</a> and border areas of <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">Londonderry</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Armagh" title="County Armagh">Armagh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">Down</a> were mainly nationalist, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Boundary_Commission" title="Irish Boundary Commission">Irish Boundary Commission</a> could reduce Northern Ireland to four counties or less.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Northern Ireland remained a part of the United Kingdom, albeit under a separate system of government whereby it was given its own <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">parliament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved government</a>. While this arrangement met the desires of unionists to remain part of the United Kingdom, nationalists largely viewed the partition of Ireland as an illegal and arbitrary division of the island against the will of the majority of its people. They argued that the Northern Ireland state was neither legitimate nor democratic, but created with a deliberately <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandered</a> unionist majority. Catholics initially composed about 35% of its population.<sup id="cite_ref-familiesatwar_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-familiesatwar-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A total of 557 people, mostly Catholics, were killed in political or sectarian violence from 1920 to 1922 in the six counties that would become Northern Ireland, both during and after the Irish War of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The result was<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> communal strife between Catholics and Protestants, with some historians describing this violence, especially that in <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogrom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> although historian Peter Hart argues that the term is not appropriate given the reciprocity of violence in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="1922.E2.80.931966"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1922\xe2\x80\x931966">1922\xe2\x80\x931966</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1922\xe2\x80\x931966">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Craig_Viscount_Craigavon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black-and-white picture of a man with a moustache." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/James_Craig_Viscount_Craigavon.jpg" width="140" height="140" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="140" data-file-height="140" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Craig_Viscount_Craigavon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Craig" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir James Craig">Sir James Craig</a>, 1st Prime Minister of Northern Ireland who said, "All I boast is that we are a Protestant Parliament and Protestant State".</div></div></div>\n<p>A marginalised remnant of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%931969)" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x931969)">Irish Republican Army</a> survived the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Irish Civil War</a>. This would come to have a major impact on Northern Ireland. Although the IRA was proscribed on both sides of the new <a href="/wiki/Irish_border" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish border">Irish border</a>, it remained ideologically committed to overthrowing both the Northern Ireland and the Free State governments by force of arms to unify Ireland. The <a href="/wiki/Executive_Committee_of_the_Privy_Council_of_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive Committee of the Privy Council of Northern Ireland">government of Northern Ireland</a> passed the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Authorities_(Special_Powers)_Act_(Northern_Ireland)_1922" title="Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act (Northern Ireland) 1922">Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act (Northern Ireland) 1922</a>; this gave sweeping powers to the government and police to do virtually anything seen as necessary to re-establish or preserve law and order. The Act continued to be used against nationalists long after the violence of this period had come to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two sides\' positions became strictly defined following this period. From a unionist perspective, Northern Ireland\'s nationalists were inherently disloyal and determined to force unionists into a united Ireland. This threat was seen as justifying preferential treatment of unionists in housing, employment and other fields. The prevalence of larger families and thus the potential for a more rapid population growth among Catholics was seen as a threat. Unionist governments ignored <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Edward Carson</a>\'s warning in 1921 that alienating Catholics would make Northern Ireland inherently unstable. After the early 1920s, there were occasional incidents of sectarian unrest in Northern Ireland. These included severe rioting in Belfast in the 1930s and 1950s, and the IRA\'s brief <a href="/wiki/Northern_Campaign_(IRA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Campaign (IRA)">Northern Campaign</a> in the 1940s and <a href="/wiki/Border_Campaign_(IRA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Border Campaign (IRA)">Border Campaign</a> between 1956 and 1962, which did not enjoy broad popular support among nationalists. After the IRA called off its campaign in 1962, Northern Ireland became relatively stable for a brief period.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_1960s">Late 1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Late 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Northern_Ireland_Troubles_and_peace_process" title="Timeline of the Northern Ireland Troubles and peace process">Timeline of the Northern Ireland Troubles and peace process</a></div>\n<p>There is little agreement on the exact date of the start of the Troubles. Different writers have suggested different dates. These include the formation of the modern <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> in 1966,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> the civil rights march in Derry on 5 October 1968, the beginning of the \'<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bogside" title="Battle of the Bogside">Battle of the Bogside</a>\' on 12 August 1969 or the deployment of British troops on 14 August 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_campaign_and_unionist_backlash">Civil rights campaign and unionist backlash</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Civil rights campaign and unionist backlash">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_civil_rights_movement" title="Northern Ireland civil rights movement">Northern Ireland civil rights movement</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_mural,_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg/220px-Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg/330px-Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg/440px-Civil_Rights_mural%2C_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_mural,_Bogside_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1159245.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A civil rights mural in Derry</div></div></div>\n<p>In the mid-1960s, a non-violent <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_civil-rights_movement_(1960s)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland civil-rights movement (1960s)">civil rights campaign</a> began in Northern Ireland. It comprised groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> (NICRA), the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Social_Justice" title="Campaign for Social Justice">Campaign for Social Justice</a> (CSJ), the Derry Citizens\' Action Committee (DCAC) and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democracy_(Ireland)" title="People&#39;s Democracy (Ireland)">People\'s Democracy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NICRA_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NICRA-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> whose stated goals were:\n</p>\n<ul><li>an end to job discrimination \xe2\x80\x93 it showed evidence that Catholics/nationalists were less likely to be given certain jobs, especially government jobs</li>\n<li>an end to discrimination in housing allocation \xe2\x80\x93 it showed evidence that unionist-controlled local councils allocated housing to Protestants ahead of Catholics/nationalists</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/One_man,_one_vote" title="One man, one vote">one man, one vote</a> \xe2\x80\x93 in Northern Ireland, only householders could vote in local elections, while in the rest of the United Kingdom all adults could vote</li>\n<li>an end to <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandering</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district" title="Electoral district">electoral boundaries</a> \xe2\x80\x93 this meant that nationalists had less voting power than unionists, even where nationalists were a majority</li>\n<li>reform of the police force (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a>) \xe2\x80\x93 it was over 90% Protestant and criticised for <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">sectarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a></li>\n<li>repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Authorities_(Special_Powers)_Act_(Northern_Ireland)_1922" title="Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act (Northern Ireland) 1922">Special Powers Act</a> \xe2\x80\x93 this allowed police to search without a warrant, arrest and imprison people without charge or trial, ban any assemblies or parades, and ban any publications; the Act was used almost exclusively against nationalists<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tonge_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tonge-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-joint2005p110_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joint2005p110-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Some suspected and accused NICRA of being a republican front-group whose ultimate goal was to unite Ireland. Although republicans and some members of the IRA (then led by <a href="/wiki/Cathal_Goulding" title="Cathal Goulding">Cathal Goulding</a> and pursuing a non-violent agenda) helped to create and drive the movement, they did not control it and were not a dominant faction within it.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cameron_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cameron-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March and April 1966, Irish nationalists/republicans held parades throughout Ireland to mark the 50th anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a>. On 8 March, a group of Irish republicans dynamited <a href="/wiki/Nelson%27s_Pillar" title="Nelson&#39;s Pillar">Nelson\'s Pillar</a> in Dublin. At the time, the IRA was weak and not engaged in armed action, but some unionists warned it was about to be revived to launch another campaign against Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ptuvf_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ptuvf-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In April 1966, loyalists led by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a>, a Protestant fundamentalist preacher, founded the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Constitution_Defence_Committee" title="Ulster Constitution Defence Committee">Ulster Constitution Defence Committee</a> (UCDC). It set up a paramilitary-style wing called the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestant_Volunteers" title="Ulster Protestant Volunteers">Ulster Protestant Volunteers</a> (UPV)<sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> to oust <a href="/wiki/Terence_O%27Neill,_Baron_O%27Neill_of_the_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Terence O&#39;Neill, Baron O&#39;Neill of the Maine">Terence O\'Neill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Prime Minister of Northern Ireland">Prime Minister of Northern Ireland</a>. Although O\'Neill was a unionist, they viewed him as being too \'soft\' on the civil rights movement and opposed his policies.<sup id="cite_ref-tricolour_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tricolour-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_murals_AB.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Belfast_murals_AB.jpg/220px-Belfast_murals_AB.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Belfast_murals_AB.jpg/330px-Belfast_murals_AB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Belfast_murals_AB.jpg/440px-Belfast_murals_AB.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_murals_AB.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A UVF mural in Belfast</div></div></div>\n<p>At the same time, a loyalist group calling itself the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a> (UVF) emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Shankill_Road" title="Shankill Road">Shankill</a> area of Belfast. It was led by <a href="/wiki/Gusty_Spence" title="Gusty Spence">Gusty Spence</a>, a former British soldier. Many of its members were also members of the UCDC and UPV.<sup id="cite_ref-jordan_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jordan-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In April and May it <a href="/wiki/Petrol_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrol bomb">petrol bombed</a> a number of Catholic homes, schools and businesses. A firebomb killed an elderly Protestant widow, Matilda Gould.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> On 21 May, the UVF issued a statement declaring "war" against the IRA and anyone helping it.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> On 27 May the UVF fatally shot a Catholic civilian, John Scullion, as he walked home. A month later it shot three Catholic civilians as they left a pub, killing a young Catholic from the Republic, Peter Ward.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after, the UVF was proscribed (made illegal) by the Northern Ireland government.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1800_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1800-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> (NICRA) was formed in January 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-NICRA_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NICRA-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 20 June 1968, civil rights activists (including <a href="/wiki/Austin_Currie" title="Austin Currie">Austin Currie</a>, a nationalist <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MP</a>) protested against housing discrimination by <a href="/wiki/Squatting" title="Squatting">squatting</a> in a house in <a href="/wiki/Caledon,_County_Tyrone" title="Caledon, County Tyrone">Caledon</a>. The local council had allocated the house to an unmarried 19-year-old Protestant (Emily Beattie, the secretary of a local UUP politician) instead of either of two large Catholic families with children.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1968_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1968-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> RUC officers \xe2\x80\x93 one of whom was Beattie\'s brother \xe2\x80\x93 forcibly removed the activists.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1968_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1968-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Two days before the protest, the two Catholic families who had been squatting in the house next door were removed by police.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Currie had brought their grievance to the local council and to Stormont, but had been told to leave. The incident invigorated the civil rights movement.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_Commemoration,_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg/220px-Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg/330px-Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg/440px-Civil_Rights_Commemoration%2C_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_Commemoration,_Coalisland_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1413288.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A monument to Northern Ireland\'s first civil rights march</div></div></div>\n<p>On 24 August 1968, the civil rights movement held its first civil rights march, from <a href="/wiki/Coalisland" title="Coalisland">Coalisland</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dungannon" title="Dungannon">Dungannon</a>. Many more marches were held over the following year. Loyalists (especially members of the UPV) attacked some of the marches and held counter-demonstrations in a bid to get the marches banned.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1968_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1968-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Because of the lack of police reaction to the attacks, nationalists saw the RUC, almost wholly Protestant, as backing the loyalists and allowing the attacks to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> On 5 October 1968, a civil rights march in Derry was banned by the Northern Ireland government.<sup id="cite_ref-derrymarch_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derrymarch-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> When marchers defied the ban, RUC officers surrounded the marchers and beat them indiscriminately and without provocation. More than 100 people were injured, including a number of nationalist politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-derrymarch_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derrymarch-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The incident was filmed by television news crews and shown around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-cathcart_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cathcart-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> It caused outrage among Catholics and nationalists, sparking two days of rioting in Derry between nationalists and the RUC.<sup id="cite_ref-derrymarch_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-derrymarch-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few days later, a student civil rights group \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democracy_(Ireland)" title="People&#39;s Democracy (Ireland)">People\'s Democracy</a> \xe2\x80\x93 was formed in Belfast.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1968_77-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1968-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In late November, O\'Neill promised the civil rights movement some concessions, but these were seen as too little by nationalists and too much by loyalists. On 1 January 1969, People\'s Democracy began a four-day march from Belfast to Derry, which was repeatedly harassed and attacked by loyalists. At <a href="/wiki/Burntollet_Bridge_incident" title="Burntollet Bridge incident">Burntollet Bridge</a> the marchers were attacked by about 200 loyalists, including some off-duty police officers, armed with iron bars, bricks and bottles in a pre-planned ambush. When the march reached Derry City it was again attacked. The marchers claimed that police did nothing to protect them and that some officers helped the attackers.<sup id="cite_ref-burntollet_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burntollet-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> That night, RUC officers went on a rampage in the <a href="/wiki/Bogside" title="Bogside">Bogside</a> area of Derry, attacking Catholic homes, attacking and threatening residents, and hurling sectarian abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-burntollet_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burntollet-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Residents then sealed off the Bogside with barricades to keep the police out, creating "<a href="/wiki/Free_Derry" title="Free Derry">Free Derry</a>", which was briefly a <a href="/wiki/No-go_area" title="No-go area">no-go area</a> for the security forces.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In March and April 1969, loyalists bombed water and electricity installations in Northern Ireland, blaming them on the dormant IRA and elements of the civil rights movement. Some attacks left much of Belfast without power and water. Loyalists hoped the bombings would force O\'Neill to resign and bring an end to any concessions to nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1969_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1969-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> There were six bombings between 30 March and 26 April.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1969_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1969-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-psb_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> All were widely blamed on the IRA, and British soldiers were sent to guard installations. Unionist support for O\'Neill waned, and on 28 April he resigned as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1969_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1969-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="August_1969_riots_and_aftermath">August 1969 riots and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: August 1969 riots and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1969_Northern_Ireland_riots" title="1969 Northern Ireland riots">1969 Northern Ireland riots</a></div>\n<p>On 19 April there were clashes between NICRA marchers, the RUC and loyalists in the Bogside. RUC officers entered the house of Samuel Devenny (42), an uninvolved Catholic civilian, and ferociously beat him along with two of his teenage daughters and a family friend.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1969_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1969-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> One of the daughters was beaten unconscious as she lay recovering from surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Devenny suffered a heart attack and died on 17 July from his injuries. On 13 July, RUC officers beat a Catholic civilian, Francis McCloskey (67), during clashes in <a href="/wiki/Dungiven" title="Dungiven">Dungiven</a>. He died of his injuries the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-cain1969_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain1969-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 August, the loyalist <a href="/wiki/Apprentice_Boys_of_Derry" title="Apprentice Boys of Derry">Apprentice Boys of Derry</a> were allowed to march along the edge of the Bogside. Taunts and missiles were exchanged between the loyalists and nationalist residents. After being bombarded with stones and petrol bombs from nationalists, the RUC, backed by loyalists, tried to storm the Bogside. The RUC used <a href="/wiki/CS_gas" title="CS gas">CS gas</a>, armoured vehicles and water cannons, but were kept at bay by hundreds of nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The continuous fighting, which became known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bogside" title="Battle of the Bogside">Battle of the Bogside</a>, lasted for two days.\n</p><p>In response to events in Derry, nationalists held protests at RUC bases in Belfast and elsewhere. Some of these led to clashes with the RUC and attacks on RUC bases. In Belfast, loyalists responded by invading nationalist districts, burning houses and businesses. There were gun battles between nationalists and the RUC, and between nationalists and loyalists. A group of about 30 IRA members was involved in the fighting in Belfast. The RUC deployed <a href="/wiki/Shorland_armoured_car" title="Shorland armoured car">Shorland armoured cars</a> mounted with heavy <a href="/wiki/M1919_Browning_machine_gun" title="M1919 Browning machine gun">Browning machine guns</a>. The Shorlands twice opened fire on a block of flats in a nationalist district, killing a nine-year-old boy, Patrick Rooney. RUC officers opened fire on rioters in <a href="/wiki/Armagh" title="Armagh">Armagh</a>, Dungannon and Coalisland.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the riots, on 13 August, <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lynch" title="Jack Lynch">Jack Lynch</a> made a television address. He condemned the RUC and said that the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland" title="Government of Ireland">Irish Government</a> "can no longer stand by and see innocent people injured and perhaps worse". He called for a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_peacekeeping" title="United Nations peacekeeping">United Nations peacekeeping</a> force to be deployed and said that <a href="/wiki/Irish_Army" title="Irish Army">Irish Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Field_hospital" title="Field hospital">field hospitals</a> were being set up at the border in <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">County Donegal</a> near Derry. Lynch added that Irish re-unification would be the only permanent solution. Some interpreted the speech as a threat of military intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> After the riots, Lynch ordered the Irish Army to plan for a possible <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_intervention" title="Humanitarian intervention">humanitarian intervention</a> in Northern Ireland. The plan, <a href="/wiki/Exercise_Armageddon" title="Exercise Armageddon">Exercise Armageddon</a>, was rejected and remained <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">classified</a> for thirty years.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 14\xe2\x80\x9315 August, British troops <a href="/wiki/Operation_Banner" title="Operation Banner">were deployed</a> in Derry and Belfast to restore order,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> but did not try to enter the Bogside, bringing a temporary end to the riots. Eight people had been shot dead, more than 750 had been injured (including 133 who suffered gunshot wounds) and more than 400 homes and businesses had been destroyed (83% Catholic-owned)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. 1,505 Catholic and 315 Protestant families were forced to flee their homes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Irish Army set up refugee camps in the Republic near the border. Nationalists initially welcomed the British Army, as they did not trust the RUC. Despite the British government\'s attempt to do "nothing that would suggest partiality to one section of the community" and the improvement of the relationship between the Army and the local population following the Army assistance with flood relief in August 1970, the Falls curfew and a situation that was described at the time as "an inflamed sectarian one, which is being deliberately exploited by the IRA and other extremists" meant that relations between the Catholic population and the British Army rapidly deteriorated .<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the riots, the \'<a href="/wiki/Hunt_Report" title="Hunt Report">Hunt Committee</a>\' was set up to examine the RUC. It published its report on 12 October, recommending that the RUC become an unarmed force and the B Specials be disbanded. That night, loyalists took to the streets of Belfast in protest at the report. During violence in the Shankill, UVF members shot dead RUC officer Victor Arbuckle. He was the first RUC officer to be killed during the Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In October and December 1969, the UVF carried out a number of small bombings in the Republic of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_peaks_and_Stormont_collapses">Violence peaks and Stormont collapses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Violence peaks and Stormont collapses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform"
