title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1971 <a href="/wiki/Newsreel" title="Newsreel">newsreel</a> about the background of the conflict</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shankilltroubles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shankilltroubles.jpg/220px-Shankilltroubles.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shankilltroubles.jpg/330px-Shankilltroubles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Shankilltroubles.jpg/440px-Shankilltroubles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shankilltroubles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Loyalist banner and graffiti on a building in the <a href="/wiki/Shankill_Road" title="Shankill Road">Shankill</a> area of Belfast, 1970</div></div></div>\n<p>1970 through to 1972 saw an explosion of <a href="/wiki/Political_violence" title="Political violence">political violence</a> in Northern Ireland, peaking in 1972, when nearly 500 people, just over half of them civilians, lost their lives. 1972 saw the greatest loss of life throughout the entire conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of 1971, 29 barricades were in place in <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>, blocking access to what was known as <a href="/wiki/Free_Derry" title="Free Derry">Free Derry</a>; 16 of these were impassable even to the British Army\'s one-ton armoured vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-b83_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b83-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Many of the nationalist/republican "<a href="/wiki/No-go_area" title="No-go area">no-go areas</a>" were controlled by one of the two factions of the Irish Republican Army\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional IRA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Official_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Official Irish Republican Army">Official IRA</a>. There are several reasons offered for why violence escalated in these years.\n</p><p>Unionists claim the main reason was the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (Provisional IRA), and the <a href="/wiki/Official_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Official Irish Republican Army">Official Irish Republican Army</a> (Official IRA), particularly the former. These two groups were formed when <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army_(1922%E2%80%931969)#The_1960s:_Marxist_tendency_and_the_1969_split" title="Irish Republican Army (1922\xe2\x80\x931969)">the IRA split into the \'Provisional\' and \'Official\' factions</a>. While the older IRA had embraced non-violent civil agitation,<sup id="cite_ref-bishopmallie_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bishopmallie-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> the new Provisional IRA was determined to wage "armed struggle" against British rule in Northern Ireland. The new IRA was willing to take on the role of "defenders of the Catholic community",<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> rather than seeking working-class ecumenical unity across both communities.\n</p><p>Nationalists pointed to a number of events in these years to explain the upsurge in violence. One such incident was the <a href="/wiki/Falls_Curfew" title="Falls Curfew">Falls Curfew</a> in July 1970, when 3,000 troops imposed a curfew on the nationalist Lower Falls area of Belfast, firing more than 1,500 rounds of ammunition in gun battles with the Official IRA and killing four people. Another was the 1971 introduction of internment without trial (of 350 initial detainees, none were Protestants).<sup id="cite_ref-rkwalker_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rkwalker-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, due to poor intelligence,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> very few of those interned were actually republican activists at the time, but some internees became increasingly radicalised as a result of their experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bloody_Sunday">Bloody Sunday</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bloody Sunday">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A third event "<a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a>", was the shooting dead of thirteen unarmed male civilians by the British Army at a proscribed anti-internment rally in Derry on 30 January 1972 (a fourteenth man died of his injuries some months later) while more than fourteen<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (November 2017)">quantify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> other civilians were wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The march had been organised by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> (NICRA). The soldiers involved were members of the <a href="/wiki/1st_Battalion,_Parachute_Regiment" title="1st Battalion, Parachute Regiment">1st Battalion, Parachute Regiment</a>, also known as "1 Para".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This was one of the most prominent events that occurred during the Northern Irish Conflict as it was recorded as the largest number of people killed in a single incident during the period.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bloody Sunday greatly increased the hostility of Catholics and Irish nationalists towards the British military and government while significantly elevating tensions during the Northern Irish Conflict. As a result, the Provisional Irish Republican Army (IRA) gained more support, especially through rising numbers of recruits in the local areas.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the introduction of internment there were numerous gun battles between the British army and both the Provisional and Official IRA. Between 1971 and 1975, 1,981 people were interned; 1,874 were Catholic/republican, while 107 were Protestant/loyalist.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> There were widespread allegations of abuse and even <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> of detainees,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-asmathews_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asmathews-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> and in 1972, the "<a href="/wiki/Five_techniques" title="Five techniques">five techniques</a>" used by the police and army for interrogation were ruled to be illegal following a British government inquiry.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Provisional IRA, or "Provos", as they became known, sought to establish itself as the defender of the nationalist community.<sup id="cite_ref-moloney_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moloney-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The Official IRA (OIRA) began its own armed campaign in reaction to the ongoing violence. The Provisional IRA\'s offensive campaign began in early 1971 when the Army Council sanctioned attacks on the British Army.<sup id="cite_ref-English137_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English137-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1972, the Provisional IRA killed approximately 100 members of the security forces, wounded 500 others, and carried out approximately 1,300 bombings,<sup id="cite_ref-bobrien_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bobrien-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> mostly against commercial targets which they considered "the artificial economy".<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-English137_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English137-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The bombing campaign killed many civilians, notably on <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Friday_(1972)" title="Bloody Friday (1972)">Bloody Friday</a> on 21 July, when 22 bombs were set off in the centre of Belfast, killing seven civilians and two soldiers.\n</p><p>In the same year, the Official IRA killed dozens of soldiers and wounded several more, mostly through gun attacks, according to the CAIN project\'s Sutton database. The Official IRA called off its campaign in May 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British troop concentrations peaked at 20:1000 of the civilian population, the highest ratio found in the history of <a href="/wiki/Counterinsurgency" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterinsurgency">counterinsurgency</a> warfare, higher than that achieved during the "<a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a>"/"Anti-British National Liberation War", which the conflict is frequently compared to.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Operation_Motorman" title="Operation Motorman">Operation Motorman</a>, the military operation for the surge, was the biggest military operation in Ireland since the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CAIN1972_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN1972-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In total, almost 22,000 British forces were involved,<sup id="cite_ref-CAIN1972_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN1972-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the days before 31 July, about 4,000 extra troops were brought into Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-CAIN1972_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN1972-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite a temporary ceasefire in 1972 and talks with British officials, the Provisionals were determined to continue their campaign until the achievement of a united Ireland. The UK government in London, believing the Northern Ireland administration incapable of containing the security situation, sought to take over the control of law and order there. As this was unacceptable to the Northern Ireland Government, the British government pushed through emergency legislation (the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_(Temporary_Provisions)_Act_1972" title="Northern Ireland (Temporary Provisions) Act 1972">Northern Ireland (Temporary Provisions) Act 1972</a>) which suspended the unionist-controlled <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Stormont</a> parliament and government, and introduced "<a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_over_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct rule over Northern Ireland">direct rule</a>" from London. Direct rule was initially intended as a short-term measure; the medium-term strategy was to restore self-government to Northern Ireland on a basis that was acceptable to both unionists and nationalists. Agreement proved elusive, however, and the Troubles continued throughout the 1970s, 1980s, and the 1990s within a context of political deadlock. The existence of "no-go areas" in Belfast and Derry was a challenge to the authority of the British government in Northern Ireland, and the British Army demolished the barricades and re-established control over the areas in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Motorman" title="Operation Motorman">Operation Motorman</a> on 31 July 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunningdale_Agreement_and_UWC_strike">Sunningdale Agreement and UWC strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sunningdale Agreement and UWC strike">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In June 1973, following the publication of a British <a href="/wiki/White_Paper" class="mw-redirect" title="White Paper">White Paper</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Irish_sovereignty_referendum,_1973" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Irish sovereignty referendum, 1973">referendum</a> in March on the status of Northern Ireland, a new parliamentary body, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly,_1973" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland Assembly, 1973">Northern Ireland Assembly</a>, was established. <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_1973" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 1973">Elections</a> to this were held on 28 June. In October 1973, mainstream nationalist and unionist parties, along with the British and Irish governments, negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Sunningdale_Agreement" title="Sunningdale Agreement">Sunningdale Agreement</a>, which was intended to produce a political settlement within Northern Ireland, but with a so-called "Irish dimension" involving the Republic. The agreement provided for "power-sharing" \xe2\x80\x93 the creation of an executive containing both unionists and nationalists\xe2\x80\x94and a "Council of Ireland" \xe2\x80\x93 a body made up of ministers from Northern Ireland and the Republic, designed to encourage cross-border co-operation. The similarities between the Sunningdale Agreement and the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Belfast Agreement</a> of 1998 has led some commentators to characterise the latter as "Sunningdale for slow learners".<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x93_Ceallaigh_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x93_Ceallaigh-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> This assertion has been criticised by political scientists one of whom stated that "..there are... significant differences between them [Sunningdale and Belfast], both in terms of content and the circumstances surrounding their negotiation, implementation, and operation".<sup id="cite_ref-wilford_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilford-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Belfast, 1974</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/British_troops_investigate_a_couple_on_the_street_in_Belfast.jpg/300px-British_troops_investigate_a_couple_on_the_street_in_Belfast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/British_troops_investigate_a_couple_on_the_street_in_Belfast.jpg/400px-British_troops_investigate_a_couple_on_the_street_in_Belfast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1054" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">British troops and police investigate a couple behind the <a href="/wiki/Europa_Hotel,_Belfast" title="Europa Hotel, Belfast">Europa Hotel</a>. They were taken away.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast,_Northern_Ireland,_1974.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/200px-Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg" width="200" height="140" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/300px-Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/400px-Protestant_graffiti_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1499" data-file-height="1049" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Protestant graffiti</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast,_Northern_Ireland,_1974.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/200px-No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg" width="200" height="140" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/300px-No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg/400px-No_Entry_to_Joy_Street_in_Belfast%2C_Northern_Ireland%2C_1974.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1499" data-file-height="1049" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Belfast street signs with IRA graffiti</div></div></div></div>\n<p>Unionists were split over Sunningdale, which was also opposed by the IRA, whose goal remained nothing short of an end to the existence of Northern Ireland as part of the UK. Many unionists opposed the concept of power-sharing, arguing that it was not feasible to share power with those (nationalists) who sought the destruction of the state. Perhaps more significant, however, was the unionist opposition to the "Irish dimension" and the Council of Ireland, which was perceived as being an all-Ireland parliament-in-waiting. Remarks by a young SDLP councillor, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Logue" title="Hugh Logue">Hugh Logue</a>, to an audience at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College_Dublin" title="Trinity College Dublin">Trinity College Dublin</a> that Sunningdale was the tool "by which the Unionists will be trundled off to a united Ireland" also damaged chances of significant unionist support for the agreement. In January 1974, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Faulkner" title="Brian Faulkner">Brian Faulkner</a> was narrowly deposed as UUP leader and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Harry_West" title="Harry West">Harry West</a>. A <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">UK general election in February 1974</a> gave the anti-Sunningdale unionists the opportunity to test unionist opinion with the slogan "Dublin is only a Sunningdale away", and the result galvanised their opposition: they won 11 of the 12 seats, winning 58% of the vote with most of the rest going to nationalists and pro-Sunningdale unionists.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ultimately, however, the Sunningdale Agreement was brought down by mass action on the part of loyalist paramilitaries (primarily the Ulster Defence Association, at that time over 20,000 strong<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and workers, who formed the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Workers%27_Council" title="Ulster Workers&#39; Council">Ulster Workers\' Council</a>. They organised a <a href="/wiki/General_strike" title="General strike">general strike</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Workers%27_Council_strike" title="Ulster Workers&#39; Council strike">Ulster Workers\' Council strike</a>. This severely curtailed business in Northern Ireland and cut off essential services such as water and electricity. Nationalists argue that the British Government did not do enough to break this strike and uphold the Sunningdale initiative. There is evidence that the strike was further encouraged by <a href="/wiki/MI5" title="MI5">MI5</a>, a part of their campaign to \'disorientate\' British prime minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>\'s government.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Faced with such opposition, the pro-Sunningdale unionists resigned from the power-sharing government and the new regime collapsed. Three days into the UWC strike, on 17 May 1974, two UVF teams from the Belfast and <a href="/wiki/UVF_Mid-Ulster_Brigade" title="UVF Mid-Ulster Brigade">Mid-Ulster</a> brigades<sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> detonated <a href="/wiki/Dublin_and_Monaghan_bombings" title="Dublin and Monaghan bombings">three no-warning car bombs</a> in Dublin\'s city centre during the Friday evening rush hour, resulting in 26 deaths and close to 300 injuries. Ninety minutes later, a fourth car bomb exploded in <a href="/wiki/Monaghan" title="Monaghan">Monaghan</a>, killing seven additional people. Nobody has ever been convicted for these attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposal_of_an_independent_Northern_Ireland">Proposal of an independent Northern Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Proposal of an independent Northern Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Wilson had secretly met with the IRA in 1971 while leader of the opposition; his government in late 1974 and early 1975 again met with the IRA to negotiate a ceasefire. During the meetings the parties discussed the possibility of British withdrawal from an independent Northern Ireland. The failure of Sunningdale led to the serious consideration in London until November 1975 of independence. Had the withdrawal occurred \xe2\x80\x94which Wilson supported but others, including <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, opposed \xe2\x80\x93 the region would have become a separate <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">Dominion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="British Commonwealth">British Commonwealth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fitzgerald2006_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fitzgerald2006-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British negotiations with an illegal organisation angered the Irish government. It did not know their proceedings but feared that the British were considering abandoning Northern Ireland. Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Garret_FitzGerald" title="Garret FitzGerald">Garret FitzGerald</a> discussed in a memorandum of June 1975 the possibilities of orderly withdrawal and independence, <a href="/wiki/Repartition_of_Ireland" title="Repartition of Ireland">repartition of the island</a> or a collapse of Northern Ireland into civil war and anarchy. The memorandum preferred a negotiated independence as the best of the three "worst case scenarios", but concluded that the Irish government could do little.<sup id="cite_ref-fitzgerald2006_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fitzgerald2006-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish government had already failed to prevent the IRA from burning down the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_Kingdom,_Dublin" title="Embassy of the United Kingdom, Dublin">British Embassy</a> in 1972. It believed that it could not enlarge the country\'s small army of 12,500 men without negative consequences. A civil war in Northern Ireland would cause many deaths there and severe consequences for the Republic, as the public would demand that it intervene to protect nationalists. FitzGerald warned Callaghan that the failure to intervene, despite Ireland\'s inability to do so, would "threaten democratic government in the Republic", which in turn jeopardised British and European security against Communist and other foreign nations.<sup id="cite_ref-fitzgerald2006_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fitzgerald2006-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish government so dreaded the consequences of an independent Northern Ireland that FitzGerald refused to ask the British not to withdraw\xe2\x80\x94as he feared that openly discussing the issue could permit the British to proceed\xe2\x80\x94and other members of government opposed the Irish Cabinet even discussing what FitzGerald referred to as a "doomsday scenario". He wrote in 2006 that "Neither then nor since has public opinion in Ireland realised how close to disaster our whole island came during the last two years of Harold Wilson\'s premiership."<sup id="cite_ref-fitzgerald2006_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fitzgerald2006-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-1970s">Mid-1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mid-1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CW._Derry,_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG/220px-CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG" width="220" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG/330px-CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG/440px-CW._Derry%2C_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1468" data-file-height="1668" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CW._Derry,_IRSP-INLA_m%C3%B6te.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> began operations in the mid-1970s.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Merlyn_Rees" title="Merlyn Rees">Merlyn Rees</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a>, had lifted the proscription against the UVF in April 1974. In December, one month after the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_pub_bombings" title="Birmingham pub bombings">Birmingham pub bombings</a> which killed 21 people, the IRA declared a ceasefire; this would theoretically last throughout most of the following year. The ceasefire notwithstanding, sectarian killings actually escalated in 1975, along with internal feuding between rival paramilitary groups. This made 1975 one of the "bloodiest years of the conflict".<sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 31 July 1975 at Buskhill, outside <a href="/wiki/Newry" title="Newry">Newry</a>, the popular Irish cabaret band "<a href="/wiki/Miami_Showband_killings" title="Miami Showband killings">The Miami Showband</a>" was returning home to Dublin after a gig in <a href="/wiki/Banbridge" title="Banbridge">Banbridge</a> when it was ambushed by gunmen from the UVF Mid-Ulster Brigade wearing British Army uniforms at a bogus military roadside checkpoint on the main <a href="/wiki/A1_road_(Northern_Ireland)" title="A1 road (Northern Ireland)">A1 road</a>. Three of the bandmembers, two Catholics and a Protestant, were shot dead, while two of the UVF men were killed when the bomb they had loaded onto the band\'s minibus detonated prematurely. The following January, eleven Protestant workers were gunned down in <a href="/wiki/Kingsmill_massacre" title="Kingsmill massacre">Kingsmill, South Armagh</a> after having been ordered off their bus by an armed republican gang, which called itself the <a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Republican_Action_Force" title="South Armagh Republican Action Force">South Armagh Republican Action Force</a>. One man survived despite being shot 18 times, leaving ten fatalities. These killings were reportedly in retaliation to a loyalist double shooting attack against the <a href="/wiki/Reavey_and_O%27Dowd_killings" title="Reavey and O&#39;Dowd killings">Reavey and O\'Dowd families</a> the previous night.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The violence continued through the rest of the 1970s. The British Government reinstated the ban against the UVF in October 1975, making it once more an illegal organisation. When the Provisional IRA\'s December 1974 ceasefire had ended in early 1976 and it had returned to violence, it had lost the hope that it had felt in the early 1970s that it could force a rapid British withdrawal from Northern Ireland, and instead developed a strategy known as the "Long War", which involved a less intense but more sustained campaign of violence that could continue indefinitely. The <a href="/wiki/Official_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Official IRA">Official IRA</a> ceasefire of 1972, however, became permanent, and the "Official" movement eventually evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_Party_of_Ireland" title="Workers&#39; Party of Ireland">Workers\' Party</a>, which rejected violence completely. However, a splinter from the "Officials"\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a>\xe2\x80\x94continued a campaign of violence in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_1970s">Late 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Late 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By the late 1970s, <a href="/wiki/War-weariness" title="War-weariness">war-weariness</a> was visible in both communities. One manifestation of this was the formation of a group known as "<a href="/wiki/Community_for_Peace_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Community for Peace People">Peace People</a>", which won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> in 1976. The Peace People organised large demonstrations calling for an end to paramilitary violence. Their campaign lost momentum, however, after they appealed to the nationalist community to provide information on the IRA to security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-dooley_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dooley-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The decade ended with a double attack by the IRA against the British. On 27 August 1979, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Earl_Mountbatten_of_Burma" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Earl Mountbatten of Burma">Lord Mountbatten</a> while on holiday in Mullaghmore, <a href="/wiki/County_Sligo" title="County Sligo">County Sligo</a>, was killed by a bomb planted on board his boat. Three other people were also killed: Lady Brabourne, the elderly mother of Mountbatten\'s son-in-law; and two teenagers, a grandson of Mountbatten and a local boatman.<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> That same day, eighteen British soldiers, mostly members of the <a href="/wiki/Parachute_Regiment_(United_Kingdom)" title="Parachute Regiment (United Kingdom)">Parachute Regiment</a>, were killed by two remote-controlled bombs in the <a href="/wiki/Warrenpoint_ambush" title="Warrenpoint ambush">Warrenpoint ambush</a> at <a href="/wiki/Warrenpoint" title="Warrenpoint">Warrenpoint, County Down</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunger_Strike.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hunger_Strike.JPG/220px-Hunger_Strike.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hunger_Strike.JPG/330px-Hunger_Strike.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Hunger_Strike.JPG/440px-Hunger_Strike.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunger_Strike.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A republican mural in Belfast commemorating the <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">hunger strikes of 1981</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Successive British Governments, having failed to achieve a political settlement, tried to "normalise" Northern Ireland. Aspects included the removal of <a href="/wiki/Internment" title="Internment">internment</a> without trial and the removal of political status for paramilitary prisoners. From 1972 onward, paramilitaries were tried in juryless <a href="/wiki/Diplock_courts" title="Diplock courts">Diplock courts</a> to avoid intimidation of jurors. On conviction, they were to be treated as ordinary criminals. Resistance to this policy among republican prisoners led to more than 500 of them in the <a href="/wiki/Maze_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Maze prison">Maze prison</a> initiating the "<a href="/wiki/Blanket_protest" title="Blanket protest">blanket"</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dirty_protest" title="Dirty protest">"dirty"</a> protests. Their protests culminated in <a href="/wiki/Hunger_strike" title="Hunger strike">hunger strikes</a> in 1980 and 1981, aimed at the restoration of political status, as well as other concessions.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Belfast_1981.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A picture of a city street with an army vehicle in the road." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/South_Belfast_1981.jpg/220px-South_Belfast_1981.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/South_Belfast_1981.jpg/330px-South_Belfast_1981.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/South_Belfast_1981.jpg/440px-South_Belfast_1981.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1282" data-file-height="857" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Belfast_1981.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Army in South Belfast, 1981</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">1981 Irish hunger strike</a>, ten republican prisoners (seven from the Provisional IRA and three from the INLA) died of starvation. The first hunger striker to die, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Sands" title="Bobby Sands">Bobby Sands</a>, was elected to Parliament on an <a href="/wiki/Anti-H-Block" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-H-Block">Anti-H-Block</a> ticket, as was his election agent <a href="/wiki/Owen_Carron" title="Owen Carron">Owen Carron</a> following Sands\' death. The hunger strikes resonated among many nationalists; over 100,000 people<sup id="cite_ref-cainhsc_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cainhsc-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> attended Sands\' funeral mass in West Belfast and thousands attended those of the other hunger strikers. From an Irish republican perspective, the significance of these events was to demonstrate potential for a political and electoral strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the hunger strikes, Sinn F\xc3\xa9in, which had become the Provisional IRA\'s political wing,<sup id="cite_ref-cainhsc_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cainhsc-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-taylorbehindthemask_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorbehindthemask-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-toolis_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-toolis-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> began to contest elections for the first time in both Northern Ireland (as <a href="/wiki/Abstentionism" title="Abstentionism">abstentionists</a>) and in the Republic. In 1986, Sinn F\xc3\xa9in recognised the legitimacy of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_D%C3%A1il" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish D\xc3\xa1il">Irish D\xc3\xa1il</a>, which caused a small group of members to break away and form <a href="/wiki/Republican_Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Republican Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Republican Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IRA\'s "Long War" was boosted by large donations of arms from <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> in the 1980s (see <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_arms_importation" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA arms importation">Provisional IRA arms importation</a>) due to <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a>\'s anger at <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Thatcher</a>\'s government for assisting the <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Reagan</a> <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan Administration">government</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/1986_bombing_of_Libya" class="mw-redirect" title="1986 bombing of Libya">bombing of Tripoli</a>, which had killed one of Gaddafi\'s children, as well as from monies from pro-IRA partisans in the United States and elsewhere throughout the Irish diaspora. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/220px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/330px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg/440px-Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1640" data-file-height="1011" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grand-Hotel-Following-Bomb-Attack-1984-10-12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Grand_Brighton_Hotel" title="Grand Brighton Hotel">Grand Brighton Hotel</a> after the IRA <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">bomb attack</a> in October 1984</div></div></div>\n<p>The INLA was highly active in the early and mid-1980s. In 1982, it <a href="/wiki/Droppin_Well_bombing" title="Droppin Well bombing">bombed a disco</a> frequented by off-duty British soldiers, killing 11 soldiers and six civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> One of the IRA\'s most high-profile actions in this period was the <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">Brighton hotel bombing</a> on 12 October 1984, when it set off a 100-pound bomb in the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Brighton_Hotel" title="Grand Brighton Hotel">Grand Brighton Hotel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a>, where politicians including Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> were staying for the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> conference. The bomb, which exploded in the early hours of the morning, killed five people, including Conservative MP Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Berry" title="Anthony Berry">Anthony Berry</a> and the wife of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Secretary_to_the_Treasury" title="Parliamentary Secretary to the Treasury">Government Chief Whip</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Wakeham" title="John Wakeham">John Wakeham</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-suttonindexofdeaths_94-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suttonindexofdeaths-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and thirty-four others were injured, including Wakeham, Trade and Industry Secretary <a href="/wiki/Norman_Tebbit" title="Norman Tebbit">Norman Tebbit</a>, and Tebbit\'s wife, Margaret. Margaret Tebbit was left permanently paralysed, while her husband\'s injuries were less serious.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 February 1985 in Newry, nine RUC officers, seven Protestants and two Catholics, were killed after a mortar attack on the police station in Corry Square. The attack was planned by the IRA South Armagh Brigade and an IRA unit in Newry. Nine shells were fired from a Mark 10 mortar which was bolted onto the back of a hijacked Ford van in Crossmaglen. Eight shells overshot the station, but the ninth hit a Portakabin which was being used as a canteen. On 8 November 1987, in <a href="/wiki/Enniskillen" title="Enniskillen">Enniskillen</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">County Fermanagh</a>, a Provisional IRA <a href="/wiki/Enniskillen_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Enniskillen bombing">time bomb</a> exploded during a parade on Remembrance Day to commemorate victims of World War One. The bomb went off by a cenotaph which was at the heart of the parade. Eleven people (ten civilians, including a pregnant woman, and one serving member of the RUC) were killed and 63 were injured. Former school headmaster Ronnie Hill was seriously injured in the bombing and slipped into a coma two days later, remaining in this condition for more than a decade before his death in December 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The IRA eventually apologised for what it claimed had been a mistake and that its target had been the British soldiers parading to the memorial. The unit which carried out the bombing was disbanded. Loyalist paramilitaries responded to the bombing with revenge attacks on Catholics, mostly civilians. Another bomb had been planted at nearby <a href="/wiki/Tullyhommon" title="Tullyhommon">Tullyhommon</a> at a parallel Remembrance Day commemoration but failed to detonate.<sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Four months after the Enniskillen attacks, three IRA volunteers were shot dead at a Shell petrol station on Winston Churchill Avenue in Gibraltar, the British colony attached to the south of Spain. This became known as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Flavius" title="Operation Flavius">Operation Flavius</a>. Their funeral at Milltown Cemetery in Belfast was attacked by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Stone_(loyalist)" title="Michael Stone (loyalist)">Michael Stone</a>, a UDA member who threw grenades and fired shots as the coffin was lowered. The attack killed three people, including an IRA volunteer. Stone was jailed for life the following year but freed 11 years later under the Good Friday Agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When two British soldiers, David Howes and Derek Wood, drove into the joint funeral in Andersonstown being held for the three men killed by Stone, they were found to be armed, and were captured, taken away and shot dead. This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Corporals_killings" title="Corporals killings">Corporals killings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1980s, loyalist paramilitary groups, including the Ulster Volunteer Force, the Ulster Defence Association and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Resistance" title="Ulster Resistance">Ulster Resistance</a>, imported arms and explosives from South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-taylorloyalists_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylorloyalists-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The weapons obtained were divided between the UDA, the UVF and Ulster Resistance, although some of the weaponry (such as <a href="/wiki/Rocket-propelled_grenade" title="Rocket-propelled grenade">rocket-propelled grenades</a>) were hardly used. These killings were reportedly in response to the 1985 <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Agreement" title="Anglo-Irish Agreement">Anglo-Irish Agreement</a> which gave the <a href="/wiki/Irish_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish government">Irish government</a> a "consultative role" in the government of Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_People%27s_Liberation_Organisation" title="Irish People&#39;s Liberation Organisation">Irish People\'s Liberation Organisation</a> (IPLO), a breakaway faction of the INLA, engaged in a bloody feud against the INLA which weakened the INLA\'s presence in some areas. By 1992, the IPLO was destroyed by the Provisionals for its involvement in drug dealing thus ending the feud.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Around this time republicans alleged that a <a href="/wiki/Shoot-to-kill_policy_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Shoot-to-kill policy in Northern Ireland">"shoot-to-kill policy"</a> had been formed in Northern Ireland, in which tens of republican paramilitaries and one loyalist paramilitary were killed on active duty, usually by the SAS or RUC.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">Northern Ireland peace process</a></div>\n<p>Since the late 1980s, while the IRA continued its armed campaign, its political wing <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a>, led since 1983 by <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Adams" title="Gerry Adams">Gerry Adams</a>, sought a negotiated end to the conflict, although Adams accurately predicted that this would be a very long process. He predicted the war would last another 20 years. He conducted open talks with <a href="/wiki/John_Hume" title="John Hume">John Hume</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the SDLP leader \xe2\x80\x93 and secret talks with government officials. Loyalists were also engaged in behind-the-scenes talks to end the violence, connecting with the British and Irish governments through Protestant clergy, in particular the Presbyterian minister, Reverend <a href="/wiki/Roy_Magee" title="Roy Magee">Roy Magee</a> and Anglican Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Robin_Eames" title="Robin Eames">Robin Eames</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>When a French TV crew filmed the IRA at a training camp in Donegal, a representative for the General Headquarters Staff of the IRA was interviewed. He said the IRA would "[E]ventually sap the political will of the British government to remain in Ireland".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Escalation_in_South_Armagh">Escalation in South Armagh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Escalation in South Armagh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_South_Armagh_Brigade" title="Provisional IRA South Armagh Brigade">IRA\'s South Armagh Brigade</a> had made the countryside village of <a href="/wiki/Crossmaglen" title="Crossmaglen">Crossmaglen</a> their stronghold since the 1970s. The surrounding villages of <a href="/wiki/Silverbridge,_County_Armagh" title="Silverbridge, County Armagh">Silverbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cullyhanna" title="Cullyhanna">Cullyhanna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cullaville" title="Cullaville">Cullaville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forkhill" title="Forkhill">Forkhill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jonesborough,_County_Armagh" title="Jonesborough, County Armagh">Jonesborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creggan,_County_Armagh" title="Creggan, County Armagh">Creggan</a> were also IRA strongholds. In February 1978, a British Army Gazelle helicopter <a href="/wiki/1978_British_Army_Gazelle_downing" title="1978 British Army Gazelle downing">was shot down near Silverbridge</a>, killing Lieutenant Colonel <a href="/wiki/Ian_Corden-Lloyd" title="Ian Corden-Lloyd">Ian Corden-Lloyd</a> of the British Army.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1990s, the IRA came up with a new plan to restrict British Army foot patrols near Crossmaglen. They developed <a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Sniper_(1990%E2%80%931997)" title="South Armagh Sniper (1990\xe2\x80\x931997)">two sniper teams</a> to attack British Army and RUC patrols.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> They usually fired from an <a href="/wiki/Improvised_vehicle_armour" title="Improvised vehicle armour">improvised armoured car</a> using a .50 BMG calibre <a href="/wiki/Barrett_M82" title="Barrett M82">M82 sniper rifle</a>. Signs were put up around South Armagh reading "Sniper at Work". The snipers killed a total of nine members of the security forces: seven soldiers and two constables. The last to be killed before the Good Friday Agreement (GFA), was a British soldier, bombardier Steven Restorick.\n</p><p>The IRA had developed the capacity to attack helicopters in South Armagh and elsewhere since the 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> including the 1990 <a href="/wiki/1990_British_Army_Gazelle_shootdown" title="1990 British Army Gazelle shootdown">shootdown of a Gazelle</a> flying over the border between Tyrone and <a href="/wiki/County_Monaghan" title="County Monaghan">Monaghan</a>; there were no fatalities in that incident.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another incident involving British helicopters in South Armagh was the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Newry_Road" title="Battle of Newry Road">Battle of Newry Road</a> in September 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Two other <a href="/wiki/1994_British_Army_Lynx_shootdown" title="1994 British Army Lynx shootdown">helicopters</a>, a British Army Lynx and a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> Puma were shot down by <a href="/wiki/Barrack_buster" title="Barrack buster">improvised mortar</a> fire in 1994. The IRA set up checkpoints in South Armagh during this period, unchallenged by the security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sniperatwork.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg/220px-Sniperatwork.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg/330px-Sniperatwork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Sniperatwork.jpg 2x" data-file-width="409" data-file-height="268" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sniperatwork.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\'Sniper at Work\' sign in Crossmaglen</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_ceasefire">First ceasefire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: First ceasefire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After a prolonged period of background political manoeuvring, both loyalist and republican paramilitary groups declared ceasefires in 1994.\nThe year leading up to the ceasefires was a particularly tense one, marked by atrocities. The UDA and UVF stepped up their attacks. The IRA responded with the <a href="/wiki/Shankill_Road_bombing" title="Shankill Road bombing">Shankill Road bombing</a> in October 1993, which aimed to kill the UDA leadership, but killed eight Protestant civilian shoppers and one low-ranking UDA member, as well as one of the perpetrators, who was killed when the bomb detonated prematurely. The UDA retaliated with mass shootings in nationalist areas such as <a href="/wiki/Greysteel_massacre" title="Greysteel massacre">Greysteel</a> and Castlerock. Twelve people were killed at Greysteel and Castlerock, all but two of whom were Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 June 1994, just before the ceasefires, the Irish National Liberation Army killed a UVF member in a gun attack on the Shankill Road. In revenge, three days later, the UVF killed six civilians in a shooting at a pub in <a href="/wiki/Loughinisland" title="Loughinisland">Loughinisland, County Down</a>. The IRA, in the remaining month before its ceasefire, killed four senior loyalist paramilitaries, three from the UDA and one from the UVF. On 31 August 1994, the IRA declared a <a href="/wiki/Ceasefire" title="Ceasefire">ceasefire</a>. The loyalist paramilitaries, temporarily united in the "<a href="/wiki/Combined_Loyalist_Military_Command" title="Combined Loyalist Military Command">Combined Loyalist Military Command</a>", reciprocated six weeks later. Although these ceasefires failed in the short run, they marked an effective end to large-scale political violence, as they paved the way for the final ceasefires.<sup id="cite_ref-coogan_53-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coogan-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jbb_113-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jbb-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1995, the United States appointed <a href="/wiki/George_J._Mitchell" title="George J. Mitchell">George J. Mitchell</a> as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Special_Envoy_for_Northern_Ireland" title="United States Special Envoy for Northern Ireland">United States Special Envoy for Northern Ireland</a>. Mitchell was recognised as being more than a token envoy and someone representing a President (<a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>) with a deep interest in events.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The British and Irish governments agreed that Mitchell would chair an international commission on disarmament of paramilitary groups.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_ceasefire">Second ceasefire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Second ceasefire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 9 February 1996, less than two years after the declaration of the ceasefire, the IRA revoked it with the <a href="/wiki/1996_Docklands_bombing" title="1996 Docklands bombing">Docklands bombing</a> in the Canary Wharf area of London, killing two people, injuring 39 others,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> and causing \xc2\xa385&#160;million in damage to the city\'s financial centre. Sinn F\xc3\xa9in blamed the failure of the ceasefire on the British Government\'s refusal to begin all-party negotiations until the IRA decommissioned its weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A picture of a large office building which has been very badly damaged." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7a/South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg/220px-South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7a/South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg/330px-South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7a/South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg 2x" data-file-width="357" data-file-height="279" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Quay_bombing_-_9.2.96.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The destruction caused by the Docklands bombing in London, 1996.</div></div></div>\n<p>The attack was followed by several more, most notably the <a href="/wiki/1996_Manchester_bombing" title="1996 Manchester bombing">1996 Manchester bombing</a>, which destroyed a large area of the centre of the city on 15 June. It was the largest bomb attack in Britain since World War II. While the attack avoided any fatalities due to a telephone warning and the rapid response of the emergency services to it, over 200 people were injured in the attack, many of them outside the established cordon. The damage caused by the blast was estimated at \xc2\xa3411&#160;million. <a href="/wiki/Lance_Bombardier" class="mw-redirect" title="Lance Bombardier">Lance Bombardier</a> Stephen Restorick, the last British soldier killed before the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> (GFA) was finalised, was shot dead at a border crossing on 12 February 1997 by the "<a href="/wiki/South_Armagh_Sniper_(1990%E2%80%931997)" title="South Armagh Sniper (1990\xe2\x80\x931997)">South Armagh sniper</a>", later identified as <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Henry_McGinn" title="Bernard Henry McGinn">Bernard Henry McGinn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The IRA reinstated their ceasefire in July 1997, as negotiations for the document that became known as the Good Friday Agreement began without Sinn F\xc3\xa9in. In September of the same year Sinn F\xc3\xa9in signed <a href="/wiki/Mitchell_Principles" title="Mitchell Principles">the Mitchell Principles</a> and were admitted to the talks. The UVF was the first paramilitary grouping to split as a result of their ceasefire, spawning the <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_Volunteer_Force" title="Loyalist Volunteer Force">Loyalist Volunteer Force</a> (LVF) in 1996. In December 1997, the INLA assassinated LVF leader <a href="/wiki/Billy_Wright_(loyalist)" title="Billy Wright (loyalist)">Billy Wright</a>, leading to a series of revenge killings by loyalist groups. A group split from the Provisional IRA and formed the <a href="/wiki/Real_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Real IRA">Real IRA</a> (RIRA).<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 1998, <a href="/wiki/Omagh_bombing" title="Omagh bombing">a Real IRA bomb</a> in <a href="/wiki/Omagh" title="Omagh">Omagh</a> killed 29 civilians. This bombing discredited "<a href="/wiki/Dissident_republican" title="Dissident republican">dissident republicans</a>" and their campaigns in the eyes of many who had previously supported the Provisionals\' campaign. They became small groups with little influence, but still capable of violence.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The INLA also declared a ceasefire after the Belfast Agreement of 1998. Since then, most paramilitary violence has been directed at their "own" communities and at other factions within their organisations. The UDA, for example, has feuded with their fellow loyalists the UVF on two occasions since 2000. There have been internal struggles for power between "brigade commanders" and involvement in organised crime.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Provisional IRA members have since been accused or convicted of involvement in the killings of <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Robert_McCartney" title="Murder of Robert McCartney">Robert McCartney</a>, Matthew Burns, James Curran, and Andrew Kearney, among others.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_process">Political process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Political process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/220px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/330px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/440px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A republican mural in Belfast during the mid-1990s. It bids "safe home" (<a href="/wiki/Sl%C3%A1n_Abhaile" class="mw-redirect" title="Sl\xc3\xa1n Abhaile">Sl\xc3\xa1n Abhaile</a>) to British troops. Security normalisation was one of the key points of the Good Friday Agreement.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the ceasefires, talks began between the main political parties in Northern Ireland to establish political agreement. These talks led to the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> of 1998. This Agreement restored self-government to Northern Ireland on the basis of "power-sharing". In 1999, an executive was formed consisting of the four main parties, including Sinn F\xc3\xa9in. Other important changes included the reform of the RUC, renamed as the <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a>, which was required to recruit at least a 50% quota of Catholics for ten years, and the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Diplock_courts" title="Diplock courts">Diplock courts</a> under the Justice and Security (Northern Ireland) Act 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A security normalisation process also began as part of the treaty, which comprised the progressive closing of redundant British Army barracks, border observation towers, and the withdrawal of all forces taking part in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Banner" title="Operation Banner">Operation Banner</a> \xe2\x80\x93 including the resident battalions of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Irish_Regiment_(1992)" title="Royal Irish Regiment (1992)">Royal Irish Regiment</a> \xe2\x80\x93 that would be replaced by <a href="/wiki/19th_Light_Brigade_(United_Kingdom)" title="19th Light Brigade (United Kingdom)">an infantry brigade</a>, deployed in ten sites around Northern Ireland but with no operative role in the province.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The power-sharing Executive and Assembly were suspended in 2002, when unionists withdrew following "<a href="/wiki/Stormontgate" title="Stormontgate">Stormontgate</a>", a controversy over allegations of an IRA spy ring operating at Stormont. There were ongoing tensions about the Provisional IRA\'s failure to disarm fully and sufficiently quickly. IRA decommissioning has since been completed (in September 2005) to the satisfaction of most parties.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A feature of Northern Ireland politics since the Agreement has been the eclipse in electoral terms of parties such as the Social Democratic and Labour Party and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a>, by rival parties such as Sinn F\xc3\xa9in and the DUP. Similarly, although political violence is greatly reduced, sectarian animosity has not disappeared. Residential areas are more segregated between Catholic nationalists and Protestant unionists than ever.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Thus, progress towards restoring the power-sharing institutions was slow and tortuous. On 8 May 2007, devolved government returned to Northern Ireland. DUP leader <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a> and Sinn F\xc3\xa9in\'s <a href="/wiki/Martin_McGuinness" title="Martin McGuinness">Martin McGuinness</a> took office as First Minister and deputy First Minister, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collusion_between_British_forces_and_loyalists">Collusion between British forces and loyalists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Troubles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Collusion between British forces and loyalists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg/220px-Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg/330px-Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg/440px-Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1909" data-file-height="1273" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Collusion_is_not_an_illusion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A republican mural in Belfast with the slogan "Collusion is not an illusion"</div></div></div>\n<p>In their efforts to defeat the IRA, there were many incidents of <a href="/wiki/Collusion" title="Collusion">collusion</a> between the British state security forces (the British Army and RUC) and loyalist paramilitaries. This included soldiers and policemen taking part in loyalist attacks while off-duty, giving weapons and intelligence to loyalists, not taking action against them, and hindering police investigations. The <i><a href="/wiki/Desmond_Lorenz_de_Silva" class="mw-redirect" title="Desmond Lorenz de Silva">De Silva</a> Report</i> found that, during the 1980s, 85% of the intelligence loyalists used to target people came from the security forces.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The security forces also had <a href="/wiki/Double_agent" title="Double agent">double agents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Informant" title="Informant">informers</a> within loyalist groups who organised attacks on the orders of, or with the knowledge of, their <a href="/wiki/Agent_handling" title="Agent handling">handlers</a>. Of the 210 loyalists arrested by the <a href="/wiki/Stevens_Inquiries" title="Stevens Inquiries">Stevens Inquiries</a> team, 207 were found to be state agents or informers.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcmay2015_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcmay2015-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British Army\'s locally recruited <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Regiment" title="Ulster Defence Regiment">Ulster Defence Regiment</a> (UDR) was almost wholly Protestant.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the vetting process, some loyalist militants managed to enlist; mainly to obtain weapons, training and information.<sup id="cite_ref-cain-subversion_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cain-subversion-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> By 1990, at least 197 UDR soldiers had been convicted of loyalist terrorist offences and other serious crimes, including 19 convicted of murder.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> This was only a small fraction of those who served in it, but the proportion was higher than the regular British Army, the RUC and the civilian population.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Glenanne_gang" title="Glenanne gang">Glenanne gang</a>\xe2\x80\x94a secret alliance of loyalist militants, British soldiers and RUC officers\xe2\x80\x94carried out a string of gun and bomb attacks against nationalists in an area of Northern Ireland known as the "murder triangle".<sup id="cite_ref-cassel2006_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cassel2006-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SArmagh_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SArmagh-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> It also carried out some attacks in the Republic, killing about 120 people in total, mostly uninvolved civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-lethal-allies-conc_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lethal-allies-conc-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Cassel Report</i> investigated 76 murders attributed to the group and found evidence that soldiers and policemen were involved in 74 of those.<sup id="cite_ref-cassel4_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cassel4-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> One member, RUC officer <a href="/wiki/John_Weir_(loyalist)" title="John Weir (loyalist)">John Weir</a>, claimed his superiors knew of the collusion but allowed it to continue.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Cassel Report</i> also said some senior officers knew of the crimes but did nothing to prevent, investigate or punish.<sup id="cite_ref-cassel4_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cassel4-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Attacks attributed to the group include the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_and_Monaghan_bombings" title="Dublin and Monaghan bombings">Dublin and Monaghan bombings</a> (1974), the <a href="/wiki/Miami_Showband_killings" title="Miami Showband killings">Miami Showband killings</a> (1975) and the <a href="/wiki/Reavey_and_O%27Dowd_killings" title="Reavey and O&#39;Dowd killings">Reavey and O\'Dowd killings</a> (1976).<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Stevens_Inquiries" title="Stevens Inquiries">Stevens Inquiries</a> found that elements of the security forces had used loyalists as "proxies",<sup id="cite_ref-stevenspeople_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stevenspeople-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> who, via, double-agents and informers, had helped loyalist groups to kill targeted individuals, usually suspected republicans but civilians were also killed, intentionally and otherwise. The inquiries concluded this had intensified and prolonged the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianscandal_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianscandal-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forcesaidedloyalists_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forcesaidedloyalists-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The British Army\'s <a href="/wiki/Force_Research_Unit" title="Force Research Unit">Force Research Unit</a> (FRU) was the main agency involved.<sup id="cite_ref-stevenspeople_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stevenspeople-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brian_Nelson_(Northern_Irish_loyalist)" title="Brian Nelson (Northern Irish loyalist)">Brian Nelson</a>, the UDA\'s chief \'intelligence officer\', was a FRU agent.<sup id="cite_ref-nelsonobituary_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nelsonobituary-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Through Nelson, FRU helped loyalists target people for assassination. FRU commanders say they helped loyalists target only republican activists and prevented the killing of civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-stevenspeople_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stevenspeople-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Inquiries found evidence only two lives were saved and that Nelson/FRU was responsible for at least 30 murders and many other attacks \xe2\x80\x93 many on civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianscandal_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianscandal-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> One victim was solicitor <a href="/wiki/Pat_Finucane" title="Pat Finucane">Pat Finucane</a>. Nelson also supervised the shipping of weapons to loyalists in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-nelsonobituary_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nelsonobituary-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> From 1992 to 1994, loyalists were responsible for more deaths than republicans,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> partly due to FRU.<sup id="cite_ref-birw_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birw-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Members of the security forces tried to obstruct the Stevens investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-forcesaidedloyalists_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forcesaidedloyalists-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2007 <a href="/wiki/Police_Ombudsman_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Ombudsman for Northern Ireland">Police Ombudsman</a> report revealed that UVF members had been allowed to commit a string of terrorist offences, including murder, while working as informers for RUC Special Branch. It found that Special Branch had given informers immunity by ensuring they weren\'t caught or convicted, and blocking weapons searches.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Ombudsman <a href="/wiki/Nuala_O%27Loan" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuala O&#39;Loan">Nuala O\'Loan</a> concluded that this had led to "hundreds" of deaths<sup id="cite_ref-bbcmay2015_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcmay2015-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and said senior British Government officials pressured her into halting her investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> UVF member <a href="/wiki/Robin_Jackson" title="Robin Jackson">Robin Jackson</a> has been linked to between 50<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and 100<sup id="cite_ref-SArmagh_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SArmagh-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> killings in Northern Ireland, although he was never convicted for any.<sup
